## Page 5

Part I. Introduction
IN accordance with the Census Act (Northern Ireland) 1965, a Census of the Population was taken
on 9th October 1966. It was the fifth since the establishment of the Government of Northern Ireland,

    Censuses having been taken in 1926, 1937, 1951 and 1961. The shortening of the intercensal period to
just over 5 years stemmed from current planning needs which, when viewed against a background of
population movement and development, require firm statistics at more frequent intervals. Censuses
of the population in other parts of the United Kingdom and in the Irish Republic were taken in April
1966.
As on previous occasions, the Census was taken on a de facto basis, i.e. it included all persons,
irrespective of their usual address or nationality, who spent the night of 9th October in Northern
Ireland, and excluded Northern Ireland residents who spent that night elsewhere. Due to the shorter
intercensal period the questions were largely limited to basic enquiries covering name, relation to the
head of household, sex, date of birth, marital status, etc., and questions on education, religion, place
of birth, nationality, fertility, occupation and household arrangements which were asked at the 1961
Census were omitted. New questions, designed to assess the extent of the movement of the population
between the various administrative areas within the country, the extent to which workers commute and
the means by which they travel to work were, however, included.
Members of the Royal Ulster Constabulary again acted as enumerators, and in spite
of the inclemency of the weather, particularly during the collection period, the enumerators carried
out their duties assiduously and in a praiseworthy manner. Each Police District, with the local District
Inspector acting as Superintendent of Enumeration, was designated as a Census District and divided into
suitably sized enumeration districts. In all, there were 34 Census Districts and 1,058 enumeration
districts. Each enumerator was required to compile a summary of the population and private dwellings
in his district, and such summaries provide the basis of the figures given in this report. While the figures
are substantially accurate they are provisional in the sense that they may require alterations when
the returns have been examined in detail.
The success of the Census operation depends on help from many quarters. In this respect, the
co-operation of householders and others responsible for the completion of returns calls for special
mention. Thanks are due to the Press, the British Broadcasting Corporation and Ulster Television Ltd.,
for their valued assistance in publicising and arousing interest in the Census ; and to the clergy, school-
teachers, public libraries, Chambers of Commerce, Young Farmers' Clubs, business firms, local author-
ities, etc. for displaying Census posters and distributing copies of the booklet, " The Census of 1966 ".
Acknowledgement is also made of the help given prior to Census day by the various Government
Departments concerned, notably H.M. Stationery Office for its co-operation in the timely printing of
documents and their despatch to Census Districts.
This preliminary Report will be followed as soon as possible by a general report summarising
information on Northern Ireland as a whole and giving detailed statistical data for each County and
County Borough and for each administrative area.
W. G. NICHOLL,
Registrar General.
GENERAL REGISTER OFFICE
FERMANAGH HOUSE
ORMEAU AVENUE
BELFAST 2
March 1967
3

## Page 6

Changes in Administrative Areas
THE following changes in the name, constitution or boundaries of administrative areas, as affecting
Census Statistics, have taken place since the date of the 1961 Census.
Co. Antrim
(1) The town of Antrim ceased to be a town created under the Towns Improvement (Ireland)
Act, 1854 and the functions of the Town Commissioners were transferred to the Rural District
Council of Antrim (1st April 1962).
(2) The District Electoral Divisions of Ballygomartin and Malone in the Rural District of Lisburn
were abolished and seven new District Electoral Divisions created, viz., Ardmore, Andersons-
town, Ballygammon, Finaghy, Ladybrook, Suffolk and Upper Malone (13th September 1963).
(3) Lisburn Urban District was made a Municipal Borough (2nd June 1964).
(4) A portion of Antrim Rural District was transferred to Ballyclare Urban District (1st April
1965).
Co. Dawn
(1) The name of Downpatrick Rural District was changed to East Down Rural District (1st January
1962).
(2) A portion of North Down Rural District was transferred to Bangor Municipal Borough
(1st April 1962).
(3) The name of Kilkeel Rural District was changed to South Down Rural District (4th July 1966).
Co. Londonderry
(1) A portion of Coleraine Rural District was transferred to Coleraine Municipal Borough
(1st April 1962). .
Co. Tyrone
(1) A portion of Cookstown Rural District was transferred to Cookstown Urban District (1st April
1963).
(2) A portion of Omagh Rural District was transferred to Omagh Urban District (1st April 1964).
4

## Page 7

Part II. Statistical Notes
Population of Northern Ireland
A total of 1,484,770 persons was enumerated in Northern Ireland on the night of 9th October, 1966.
Of this number 724,477 were males and 760,293 were females. The total is the highest recorded at any
Census since that of 1841, and continues the upward trend which has been in evidence since 1901. This
actual (or de facto) population included all persons—civilian and Service personnel—enumerated on
land, in vessels on inland waters or in ports or at anchorages and persons in ships on coastwise
voyages to Northern Ireland on Census night.
At the Census in 1961, the population totalled 1,425,042 persons. During the intercensal period
of just over five years there was, therefore, an increase of 59,728 or 4.2 per cent. The average annual

    increase between the 1961 and 1966 Censuses was 10,933, compared with an average yearly increase of
    5,390 for the intercensal period 1951-1961.
Table 1 affords a comparison of the population of Northern Ireland at each Census from 1821

    and of the intercensal variations. In comparing the figures for the 1926, 1937 and 1951 Censuses, it
should be noted that these enumerations were taken at irregular intervals whereas the other Censuses,
except that for 1966, were taken decennially.
Natural Increase and Intercensal Movement
The figures for natural increase, i.e. excess of births over deaths, are obtained from the annual
statistics of births and deaths published in the Registrar General's Annual Reports. The natural in-
crease of the population for each intercensal period since birth and death registration commenced in
1864 is shown in Table A, together with the actual variations in population for each period and the net
loss by migration.

The increase in the population, on an annual average basis, is the highest for any intercensal
period since 1821-31. It will also be seen from Table A that this larger addition to the population is

    directly attributable to a rise in the flow of births. In the intercensal period 1961-66 there were 182,529
live births, which is considerably higher (allowing for the different time intervals) than the number for
any intercensal period since that for the period 1871-1881.
The annual average number of deaths during the period 1961-66 was only slightly greater than that
for the intercensal period 1951-61. Thus, with deaths generally stable and births increased the natural
increase (excess of live births over deaths) has risen from a yearly average of 11,232 for the period

    1951-61 to 17,843 for the latest intercensal period, and is the highest ever recorded since the introduction
of birth and death registration.
The loss by migration may be deduced by subtraction of the intercensal increase from the

    natural increase. In the five years 1961-66 the net loss by migration amounted to 37,751, which
offsets to some degree the gain resulting from the natural increase. This net loss by migration represents

    an annual average of 6,910. The comparable annual averages for the periods 1951-61 and 1937-51
    were 9,185 and 4,768 respectively, but it should be noted that the position in the 1937-51 period was
affected by the second world war when migration abroad was largely suspended.

Population of Administrative Areas
Table C shows that the aggregate population of the 36 urban areas (including the county boroughs
of Belfast and Londonderry) amounted to 790,005 persons, while the population of the 31 rural districts
totalled 694,765, the proportions of the population now living in urban and rural districts being 53.2
and 46.8 per cent respectively.

It will be seen that of the total population, the proportion residing in urban areas has declined from

    54.0 per cent to 53.2 per cent since 1961, and that in the rural section the complementary increase has
raised the proportion from 46.0 to 46.8 per cent. It is noteworthy that it is the first occasion since such
figures were compiled that the rural section has gained at the expense of the urban areas. It should,
however, be remembered that some rural districts, e.g. Castlereagh and Lisburn, contain substantial
portions which are urban in character.
Table 3 gives details of the distribution of population by each administrative area at the 1966
Census with comparable figures for 1961. Where changes were made in the boundaries of areas during
the intercensal period, the 1961 figures have been adjusted to take account of these changes ; for that
reason, the figures for the aggregates of urban and rural areas differ somewhat from the corresponding
1961 figures in Table C.
As was the case at the 1961 Census, the population of each County increased except that for County
Fermanagh which showed a decrease of 3.2 per cent. Increases were proportionally greatest in Counties
Antrim and Down ; 14.5 and 7.5 per cent respectively, and lowest in Counties Armagh (6.3 per cent)
and Tyrone (1.3 per cent). Londonderry County Borough increased by 3.6 per cent and now has a
population of 55,681. The population of Belfast County Borough, which decreased by 6.3 per cent

    during the ten year period 1951-61, shows a further reduction of 16,586 persons or 4.0 per cent over the
latest intercensal period. The decrease, however, is more than offset by the increase in the population of
the administrative areas adj oining Belfast, which, no doubt, reflects the movement of persons from the City
6


---

## Page 9

to new housing estates in these areas. Table 4 shows the population of Belfast County Borough and its
immediate environs, the latter having been taken as Newtownabbey Urban District and those portions
of the Rural Districts of Castlereagh, Hillsborough and Lisburn which comprise continuous built-up
areas extending from the City boundaries. It will be seen that the population of this urbanised area

    amounts to 549,939 persons, an increase of 20,402 or 3.9 per cent since the 1961 Census.
Of the 34 urban areas (excluding the two County Boroughs), 29 show increases in population
during the period 1961-66. The largest increases were recorded in Newtownabbey Urban District

    (+ 9,746 or 26.0 per cent), Lisburn Municipal Borough (+ 3,838 or 21.7 per cent) and Lurgan Municipal
Borough (+2,805 or 15.7 per cent). The five urban areas in which decreases were recorded are Holywood

    (—274), Enniskillen (—252), Newry (—215), Newtownards (—155) and Dromore (—43).
    Of the 31 Rural Districts, 17 show increases and 14 show decreases. The districts that showed the
greatest increases were those that border on Belfast County Borough, viz. Lisburn, Castlereagh and

    Hillsborough Rural Districts for which, respectively, increases of 15,744 (30.8 per cent), 9,840
    (26.3 per cent) and 4,887 (18.1 per cent) were recorded.
Towns of Undefined Boundary
Table 5 shows details of the population of 200 identifiable towns or villages which have no legally
defined boundaries but contained 50 or more houses at the 1966 Census. Two of these towns, Antrim
and Greenisland, had populations of over 5,000. Bessbrook, Comber and Magherafelt each had popula-
tions in excess of 3,000. Populations of over 2,000 were recorded in each of the towns of Randalstown,
Ballynahinch, Killyleagh and Coalisland, and a further 23 towns had populations of 1,000 and
over. Housing estates or clusters of houses adjoining administrative urban areas and which are regarded
as extensions of the urban areas are not included in Table 5.
Sexes
There were 35,816 more females than males enumerated at the 1966 Census. The female preponder-
ance is slightly smaller than the corresponding female excess of 36,594 enumerated in 1961, and represents

    a ratio of 1,049 females per 1,000 males, compared with a ratio of 1,053 females per 1,000 males recorded
at each of the last two Censuses. Over the years the female/male ratio has been declining, and this is
mainly due to the preponderance of male births enhanced by a reduced infant mortality, particularly
in respect of males.
The sex distribution differs widely in different parts of the country. High female/male ratios are
found in those areas attractive to the retired and elderly, among whom women, with their greater
longevity, are much more numerous, and in those areas where employment of women is a predominant
factor. Areas with low ratios are mainly agricultural which would indicate that more women than
men tend to leave these areas for employment in larger towns.
The following statement analyses the figures in Table A by sex for the latest intercensal period.

Inhabited Private Dwellings and Private Households
Table 6 affords a comparison of the numbers of inhabited private dwellings and private housholds
accommodated therein at the 1961 and 1966 Censuses by administrative areas. During this intercensal
period the number of inhabited private dwellings in Northern Ireland increased by 28,494 or 7.7 per cent,
and the number of private households by 27,260 or 7.3 per cent. Increases were recorded in each of the
counties and county boroughs, Counties Antrim and Down having numerically and relatively the
greatest increases. Increases in Counties Armagh and Londonderry were also relatively high, being
above those for Northern Ireland as a whole. The administrative areas within these counties for which
the greatest increases were recorded are those which border on Belfast County Borough. For all urban
areas, increases of 6.7 per cent and 5.9 per cent were recorded for dwellings and households respectively ;
while in the rural areas, dwellings and households each increased by 9.1 per cent.

Notes to Table 3
A portion of Antrim R.D. with a population of 2 in 1961 was transferred to Ballyclare U.D. in 1965.

    A portion of North Down R.D. with a population of 477 in 1961 was transferred to Bangor M.B. in 1962.

## Page 25

INTRODUCTION
In accordance with the Census Act (Northern Ireland) 1965, a Census of the
population was taken on 9th October, 1966. It was the fifth since the estab-
lishment of the Government of Northern Ireland, Censuses having been taken in

    1926, 1937, 1951 and 1961. The shortening of the intercensal period to just
over 5 years stemmed from the needs which, when viewed against a background of
population movement and development, require firm statistics at more frequent
intervals. Censuses in the other parts of the United Kingdom and in the Irish
Republic were taken in April, 1966. The Northern Ireland Census was a full
enumeration, while those in Great Britain were on a ten per cent sample basis.
As this was an interim census the questions were largely limited to basic
enquiries, but some new questions which were not asked at any previous census
were included. County Reports, which were published for all previous censuses,
will not be issued on this occasion. The present Report, which is the final
volume, contains figures down to administrative area level. Provision, however,
has been made to meet any special demand for population, dwelling or household
statistics for every ward in boroughs or urban districts and for every district
electoral division and townland in rural districts; a charge may be made for
this data, depending on the extent of the information required.
This Report does not review the history of census taking in Northern
Ireland. Complete accounts of earlier Censuses are contained in the relevant
General Reports and it is not considered necessary to cover this field in the
present volume.
As in the past, the success of the Census operation was dependent on help
from many quarters, most of them being mentioned in the notes on the various
fields of Census activity in Part I of the Report. Their valued assistance is
gratefully acknowledged. The actual enumeration was carried out smoothly and
expeditiously, reflecting the highest credit on the large body of enumerators
and their Superintendents for the efficient way in which they performed their
duties, and on the householders and other members of the public for their ready
co-operation in completing the Census forms.
W.G. NICHOLL
Registrar
General
General Register Office
Fermanagh House
Ormeau Avenue
BELFAST
BT2 8HX
March 1968
III

VIII

## Page 31

PART I
GENERAL NOTES ON THE CENSUS
PREPARATIONS FOR THE CENSUS
The Questions: As for previous censuses the schedule of enquiries was
drawn up in close collaboration with Northern Ireland Government Departments.
Consideration was given as to whether a sample census should be taken. Errors,
however, are inherent in sampling, and such a restriction in Northern Ireland,
with its numerous administrative areas of small population, would have seriously-
hindered the use of the census. Consequently, it was decided that the census
should cover every household but that the questions asked should be limited.
Questions on relationship to head of household, sex, date of birth,
marital status and usual residence which are stock questions and provide the
essential basic information, were included as before. In 1961 people were asked
their age in years and months but it was hoped to improve the accuracy of the
statistics by asking on this occasion for the actual date of birth. Some of the
1961 Census questions (religion, place of birth, fertility, education, occupa-
tion, tenure and household arrangements) were omitted, but a few new questions
were added.
The question on usual residence was in the same form as in 1961. The
answers were used to establish, for each administrative area, the number of
persons who were enumerated there but had a usual residence elsewhere in
Northern Ireland, and also the number of people who were enumerated elsewhere
in Northern Ireland but had a usual residence in the administrative area. This
information enabled the enumerated population to be adjusted so as to provide
a base for the annual estimates of local populations, which relate to the
resident population.
Migration questions asking the usual address at 24th April, 1965, and at
24th April, 1961, were included for the first time. They were designed to give
statistics of the number of people who changed their usual residence during the
periods from the above dates to census day, with details of sex and area of
previous residence.
Place of Work and Means of Transport to Work questions were also included
for the first time to enable tables to be constructed, showing the workplace
movement from one area to another and the main means of transport to work used
for such movement.
While a question on occupation was omitted, it was decided, having regard
to changes in employment as occasioned by new industries established in Northern
Ireland in later years, to retain one on industry. Further questions were asked
to enable a brief analysis to be given by economic position of persons aged 15
years and over.
The information required for dwellings, private households (the number
occupying the premises) and the number of rooms occupied by each household was
recorded by the enumerator on each private household return.
The full extent of the questions may be seen from the copy of the House-
hold Return reproduced at the end of this Report. The same personal questions
were asked in the other types of returns used at the census except the Special
Forces Return which excluded enquiries about relationship, usual address,
migration, workplace and transport to work, but enquired as to the branch of
the Service in which employed in lieu of the standard question on industry.
Legal Authority for the Census: The authority for the taking of the Census
and the publication of the Census Reports was contained in the Census Act
(Northern Ireland) 1965, the date of the Census being fixed by an Order in
Council on 12th January, 1966. The Census Act prescribed the persons by whom
and with respect to whom the Census returns were to be made, the substance of the
questions to be asked and also provided for the appointment of enumerators, the
making of the detailed enumeration arrangements and the strict observance of


---

## Page 32

the confidential nature of the returns. Additionally, it authorised the Ministry
of Finance to collect and publish from time to time any available statistical
information concerning Northern Ireland until the taking of the next census.
The Returns: As at previous censuses, the greater part of the enumeration
was concerned with the private household, which may be defined as one or more
persons occupying a house, flat, apartment, etc., such persons being enumerated
on the Household Returns. The duty of completing the return rested with the
head of the household. In non-private households and in institutional establish-
ments the responsibility was placed on the proprietor, manager, chief resident
officer, etc., while in Defence Establishments (including naval vessels) all
persons present on census night were enumerated by Commanding Officers, under
special arrangements made with the Ministry of Defence. Servicemen and their
families living in married quarters were enumerated as private households.
An arrangement was made, under the provisions of the Census Act, whereby
with certain exceptions an individual, who did not wish to disclose his personal
details to the person responsible for completing the return, could fill in a
separate confidential return which was then left in a sealed envelope for the
enumerator or handed to him direct. The particulars on the separate return
were subsequently copied by the enumerator into the return in which they would
normally have been included. In practice, the facility was used sparingly and
there was no evidence of its abuse.
Enumeration Districts and Enumerators: As in the past, members of the
Royal Ulster Constabulary acted as enumerators. Each police district was
designated as a census district with the local District Inspector acting as
Superintendent of Enumeration. As a preliminary step each Superintendent was
required to divide his District into enumeration districts of suitable size
having due regard to local administrative divisions. A comparison with records
maintained in the Census Office and the mapping of enumeration districts
provided a check that the entire country had been covered. In all, there were
34 census districts and 1,058 enumeration districts.
During the week preceding the period for delivery of the returns, members
of the Census Office Staff visited the various Census Districts to assist in
briefing the enumerators on their duties.
Publicity: The success of the census depends not merely upon the legal and
administrative machinery but upon the willing and understanding co-operation of
the public. The individual citizen needs to understand what the census is, what
part he has to play and that census information benefits all and injures none.
For these reasons the census was given wide publicity in the daily and
provincial press. Special talks on the census were featured in the local tele-
vision and radio current affairs programmes, and the morning after census day
a short talk was given on radio, exhorting householders to have their forms
completed and ready for collection by the enumerators. Other efforts included
the issue of 4,500 census posters for display at post offices, police stations,
schools, transport depots, libraries, other public buildings and large indus-
trial and business establishments. Almost 8,000 free copies of an explanatory
pamphlet were distributed mostly with the posters and to clergymen and such
organisations as chambers of commerce and young farmers' clubs.
THE E^fUMERATION
The distribution of returns commenced with deliveries on 28th September
to the large hospitals and institutions with an estimated resident population
of 100 or more persons. The general delivery, mainly to private households,
began on 30th September and continued as was necessary to 7th and in isolated
cases to 8th October. The collection of returns, which commenced on the morning
of lOth October, i.e., the day after Census day, had to be completed by
22nd October at the latest. The enumerators were instructed to carry out the
collection as quickly as possible, particularly of returns from ships and
caravans and from boarding houses, hotels and other premises likely to have
changing population. Special arrangements were made to help ensure that any
houseless persons, or persons who moved from one house to another between the


---

## Page 33

times of delivery and collection of returns, were not overlooked.
In the course of delivering returns, each enumerator was required to
enter in his enumeration record book such particulars as the address and brief
description of every building in his district, the name of each householder and
the type of return delivered. It was his duty to give any explanations which
were sought or which he considered advisable regarding the questions on the
returns. On collecting a return, he had to record the fact in the enumeration
book, examine the return and satisfy himself that it was properly filled up
and signed. Additionally, he had to complete the panel on each private house-
hold return relating to the number of households occupying the premises and
the number of rooms occupied by each household.
Apart from the problems of delivering and collecting returns within the
times allotted in large and heavily populated districts, the enumeration went
smoothly and favourable comments were received on the degree of co-operation
from the public generally. Many householders, however, had difficulties in
answering some of the questions, especially the rather complex enquiries about
industry and place of work, with the result that the enumerators often had to
spend an unexpectedly high proportion of their time in examining and correcting
returns at the doorstep. It is gratifying to state that no cases were reported
by enumerators of refusal to make a census return.
SCRUTINY AND CODING OF INFORMATION
On receipt of the completed volumes of returns and enumeration books in
the Census Office, a check was made that all the constituent townlands or
streets of each enumeration district had been enumerated, and that the returns
for the district were properly arranged within their cover.
For Preliminary Report purposes, the data was extracted from the summary
sheets in the enumerators* record books and no reference was made to the
schedules.
The scrutiny and revision of the returns was commenced as soon as the
first volumes came to hand. This process was designed to provide a general
check of the returns and to insert codes in the usual address and migration
questions columns. The revision section were provided with detailed instruc-
tion as to their duties, of which the more important were to decide for each
return whether it related to a private or non-private household, check the
return generally with a view to detecting errors, inconsistencies or omissions
and verify the totals in the summary at the foot of the return, and if neces-
sary to amend the figures to agree with the number of persons actually
enumerated.
If date of birth was not stated further enquiries were instituted through
the enumerators. In all 1,243 cases were referred to enumerators, and
residually the date of birth of only 52 persons was estimated by the Census
Office, having regard to information furnished by the enumerator and the other
data on the return. Where sex was omitted the information was deduced from
such details in the entry as name and relationship. Where the marital condi-
tion was omitted, such cases being comparatively rare, the other information
in the entry usually enabled this to be inserted, but in some cases it was
necessary to make an intelligent guess with regard to such factors as age,
relationship, etc.
The coding of usual residence and migration questions, the latter being
simply the address of usual residence at periods of time approximately one
year and five years previous to census date, was undertaken by the revision
section. This was a relatively simple operation in which the information given
on the return usually allowed immediate and certain allocation to the correct
code.
The coding of industry, which was the most complex of the coding processes,
was based upon the Standard Industrial Classification. To facilitate the coders
on this operation lists of employers were compiled from information supplied
by the Ministry of Commerce and the Ministry of Health and Social Services.
XI


---

## Page 34

The lists, which were prepared on a geographical basis, showed assignments to
the Minimum List Headings and sub-divisions, and contained particulars of most
industrial and commercial establishments with five or more employees.
Initially, all scrutiny and coding was checked 100 per cent. As staff
became proficient, the proportion of the work checked was gradually reduced
and in some cases fell to 20 per cent subject to the officer maintaining a
sufficiently low error rate. Overall about 50 per cent of the work was checked.
The various scrutiny, coding and related operations were integrated and
arranged to provide an ample flow of fully notated returns for final tape
punching. At the height of these operati ons the Census Office Staff, excluding
staff on tape punching duties, totalled 24 of whom 21 were clerks and clerical
assistants.
PROCESSING OF DATA
Apart from the preparation of the Preliminary Report and certain tables
in the Final Report, the figures for which were extracted clerically from the
Enumerators' records, the processing and analysis of the census data were
carried out by the Computer Services Branch of the Ministry of Finance, using
an I.C.T. Type 1905 electronic digital computer. The detailed technical
investigation, system development and programming and subsequent implementation
of the computer processes were undertaken entirely by the Computer Services
Branch.
The first operation was the preparation from the census returns of the
punched paper tape for use as input to the computer; this occupied on average,
twelve machine operators and one supervisor for a period of approximately
seven months including an initial training period of about one to two weeks.
The input data was transferred to magnetic tape, this process including
a comprehensive editing and validation of the information to eliminate the
less obvious errors, not readily evident at the clerical scrutiny or coding
stage. Some 30 odd checks were applied although, since several checks tested
for a number of possible error conditions, a total of up to 180 tests, in all,
were carried out on each record. Further significant functions of this editing
process were the association of the records for married couples enumerated
together and the automatic linking of the various households in a multiple-
household dwelling. An important aspect of the editing processing was the rigid
numeric control which was incorporated into the system; this control was
maintained and repeatedly checked by computer until it had been established
that all records had been validated and transferred to magnetic tape. It was
found that some 11,200 records, or 0.8 per cent, gave rise to at least one
query, some resulting in several; definite errors normally caused rejection of
the individual record concerned although some errors - more often those arising
for technical reasons concerned with the preparation of the punched paper
tape - could result in several records being rejected; the system made pro-
vision for the correction and re-input of these records. Records containing
information of doubtful accuracy were transferred to magnetic tape at the
initial input stage but the system provided for their subsequent amendment if
clerical investigation found this to be necessary. In a few instances, it was
found possible, by building an automatic correction procedure into the computer
programs, to recover without recourse to clerical action.
Computer processing followed to produce the required results. The most
interesting feature at this stage was the production of tabulations on an
electric typewriter from punched paper tape prepared by the computer. This
enabled each page of the statistical tables to be produced in the exact format
required for photographic reduction and reproduction for the published report,
effecting considerable savings in manual typing, editing and proof reading.
XII


---

## Page 35

PART II
EXPLANATORY NOTES AND DEFINITIONS OF
TERMS USED IN THE REPORT
AREA
The acreages given are as measured by the Ordnance Survey and relate to
the various administrative and other areas as constituted at the date of the
1966 Census. Foreshore is excluded. Acreages of areas covered by the larger
rivers, lakes and tideways are also excluded from the body of Table 4, but the
total is given in a footnote thereto.
POPULATION
The 1966 Census population comprises all living persons, irrespective of
their usual address or nationality, who spent census night in the area of
enumeration. Members of the Armed Forces (including those of Commonwealth and
foreign countries except personnel aboard foreign naval vessels) are included.
Persons travelling on census night were enumerated with the population of the
district in which they arrived the next day unless they had already been
enumerated elsewhere. Persons on vessels at moorings or anchorage in Northern
Ireland waters or aboard ships on coastwise voyage to Northern Ireland ports
were counted as part of the population of the district containing the mooring,
anchorage or arrival port.
Population figures for the 1961 Census are adjusted to relate to areas as
constituted at the date of the 1966 Census. Figures for earlier censuses
relate, generally, to areas as constituted at the respective census dates.
ADMINISTRATIVE DIVISIONS
Northern Ireland comprises six counties and two county boroughs. The
counties are divided into a total of 65 administrative areas (10 municipal
boroughs, 24 urban districts and 31 rural districts); they are also divided
into 130 county electoral divisions which form the territorial units for
county council election purposes. The two county boroughs, seven of the
municipal boroughs and ten of the urban districts are divided into wards which
are the units for elections to the respective local councils; in the case of
the remaining three municipal boroughs and fourteen urban districts, the
entire borough or urban district is the unit for municipal elections. In the
rural districts, the units for local council elections are the district
electoral divisions of which there were 611 at census day.
There are 48 territorial constituencies for ejections to the Northern
Ireland Parliament, each returning one member. Additionally, four members are
returned by the constituency of The Queen's University of Belfast. There are
twelve constituencies for elections to the United Kingdom Parliament.
The following changes, affecting census statistics, took place in the
names, constitution or boundaries of administrative areas during the last
intercensal period:
Co. Antrim
i) The town of Antrim ceased to be a town created under the Towns
Improvement (Ireland) Act, 1854 and the functions of the Town
Commissioners were transferred to the Rural District Council of
Antrim on 1st April, 1962.
XIII


---

## Page 36

(ii) The District Electoral Divisions of Ballygomartin and Malone in the
Rural District of Lisburn were abolished and seven new District
Electoral Divisions created, viz., Ardmore, Andersonstown,
Ballygammon, Finaghy, Ladybrook, Suffolk and Upper .Malone, on
13th September, 1963.
(iii) Lisburn Urban District was made a Municipal Borough on 2nd June,
1964.
(iv) A portion of Antrim Rural District, with a population of 2 in 1961,
was transferred to Ballyclare Urban District on 1st April, 1965.
Co. Down
(i) The name of Downpatrick Rural District was changed to East Down
Rural District on 1st January, 1962.
(ii) A portion of North Down Rural District, with a population of 477 in
1961, was transferred to Bangor Municipal Borough on 1st April, 1962.
(iii) The name of Kilkeel Rural District was changed to South Down Rural
District on 4th July, 1966.
Co. Londonderry
(i) A portion of Coleraine Rural District, with a population of 598 in
1961, was transferred to Coleraine Municipal Borough on 1st April,
1962.
Co. Tyrone
(i) A portion of Cookstown Rural District, with a population of 184 in
1961, was transferred to Cookstown Urban District on 1st April, 1963.
(ii) A portion of Omagh Rural District, with a population of 1,184 in
1961, was transferred to Omagh Urban District on 1st April, 1964.
BUILDINGS FOR HABITATICN
For census purposes, a building was broadly defined as a structure
comprising one or more rooms or other spaces within external or party walls.
A structure detached from, but subsidiary to, a main building was regarded as
part of the main building unless it contained residential accommodation
normally occupied by a separate household.
Non-permanent structures such as huts, shacks and converted railway
carriages and mobile structures such as tents and caravans were treated as
buildings only if they were occupied on census night. Unoccupied structures
of wood, asbestos, corrugated iron, etc., used as casual residences, e.g., at
week-ends or holiday times, were regarded as buildings if they appeared to be
of sound construction and had amenities such as piped water, sewerage
facilities and electric lighting.
Dilapidated structures which were unoccupied and unlikely to be used again
were ignored. Buildings in course of erection were listed only if some part
thereof was in use as residential accommodation.
Buildings for habitation comprise all buildings used wholly or partly as
residential accommodation. They are divided into two categories:
(i) Private dwellings were normally private dwelling houses, bungalows,
including prefabricated bungalows provided by a local authority, or
flats with front doors of their own. Where houses or other buildings
had been adapted to provide residential accommodation in smaller
units, these smaller units were regarded as dwellings if they were
XIV


---

## Page 37

structurally separate, i.e., if such a unit had a separate front
door to the street, of if access to the main door was gained by
means of a common staircase or landing. In order for such a unit to
count as a dwelling it also had to be self-contained - allowing
movement between its rooms internally without making use of the
common staircase or landing and the household arrangements, viz.,
kitchen, bathroom and water closet were not shared with the
occupants of another unit of accommodation in the building. Bed-
sitting rooms (sometimes called one room flatlets) were not counted
as separate dwellings.
In a hotel, hospital or other institution a private dwelling
was identified only if it was occupied by a private household. If
such a dwelling was occupied by members of the institution not
constituting a private household it was not counted. Married
quarters in defence establishments were counted as dwellings without
exception.
A building was regarded as not wholly residential if part of it
was used exclusively or primarily for purposes other than living
accommodation, for example, a factory or block of offices containing
a director's or caretaker's flat, or a building comprising a shop
and dwelling. Buildings such as a dentist's or doctor's house with a
room or annexe used as a surgery and a house containing a clergy-
man's or author's study were, however, treated as wholly residential.
(ii) Other buildings consisted of hotels, hostels, boarding houses,
hospitals, welfare centres, religious community premises, boarding
schools and other premises which exist for a specific functional
purpose in addition to providing residential accommodation. Civilian
shipping and establishments under Naval, Army and Air Force
discipline were included.
The term boarding house covers buildings described as such in
the census returns and any buildings returned as private dwellings
but containing five or more boarders.
The term inhabited denotes actual residence of a person or persons on
census night. Uninhabited buildings therefore include not only those which
were vacant on census night but also those from which the usual occupants
were temporarily absent. Permanent habitation means normal occupation all the
year round.
HOUSEHOLDS
A private household is a person living alone, or a group of persons living
together, partaking of meals prepared together and benefiting from a common
housekeeping. A person or persons living but not boarding with a household in
a house, flat, etc., is treated as a separate household; but a person living
with a household who usually has at least one main meal a day provided by
that household while in residence is part of that household.
A private household must have exclusive use of at least one room. If two
people share one room and do not have exclusive use of at least one other room
they are treated as one household.
Non-private households comprise all persons who were enumerated in hotels,
boarding houses, old peoples' homes and institutions which exist for a
functional purpose other than that of providing accommodation. They also
include persons in defence establishments and ships at moorings or anchorages
or on coastwise voyages. A household originally enumerated as private was
re-classified as non-private where the number of boarders or the number of
inmates, patients, staff, etc., was five or more.
XV


---

## Page 38

ROOMS
The VQOm^ counted are those normally used by the household for living
eating or sleeping purposes. A kitchen if so used is included, but not a '
scullery which is used only for washing, cleaning or cooking. A pantry, cloak-
room, landing, lobby, closet or bathroom, or any warehouse, office, shop or
other room used for non-domestic purposes is also excluded.
In the tables classifying households by rooms occupied, any room shared
by two or more households in the same dwelling is allocated wholly to one
(usually the main) household, ensuring however that the total number of rooms
in the dwelling is not altered.
VALUATION
The figures shown in Table 4 relate to the rating year 1966/67 and have
been supplied by the Commissioner of Valuation. They are the totals of the
valuations in the various areas before reduction on account of derating, but
exclude in certain cases the valuations of such miscellaneous items as
electricity, gas, trolley bus and water undertakings, telegraphs, telephones
and fisheries. The total of the excluded valuations is given in a footnote to
the Table.
Subject to the Local Government (Rating and Finance) Act (Northern
Ireland), 1929, as amended by subsequent legislation, agricultural land and
buildings are exempt from all ordinary rates, while industrial and freight-
transport hereditaments and salmon and eel fisheries are partially derated.
The consequent deficiency in the yield of rates is made good by way of
General Exchequer contributions.
USUAL ADDRESS
The census schedules (except the special schedules used for the enumera-
tion of Service personnel stationed in naval, army or air force establishments
other than those living in married quarters) contained the following question:
If this dwelling is the person's usual address write "Here"; if it is not,
write the usual address in full.
The persons regarded as having their usual address in the area of
enumeration include:
(i) Persons enumerated in their own homes, and those enumerated in and
having a settled residence in private lodgings, boarding houses and
hotels.
(ii) Resident domestic staff enumerated at their place of work.
(iii) Resident staff and persons who were shown to be permanent inmates
of homes for old people, homes for terminal care, homes for handi-
capped persons and homes for persons requiring special care.
(iv) Persons with no fixed addresses and persons for whom usual
addresses were not stated and which could not be assumed from the
census returns.
School children and students living away from home during term time
were required to give their home addresses. Members of H.M. Forces not
enumerated on the special Forces schedules had to give the addresses of their
married quarters or other home addresses. Merchant seamen enumerated on
vessels and usually living aboard ship were assigned to the countries in
which the ships were registered.
MIGRATION
In addition to stating their usual addresses on census day, all persons
were required to state their usual addresses at 24th April, 1965, and at
XVI


---

## Page 39

24th April, 1961. The information derived from these questions formed the basis
of the migration statistics published in Tables 11 and 12, which, respectively
cover the periods from the above dates to census day. The statistics relate to'
the total enumerated population allocated to their area or country of usual
residence. Members of the armed forces enumerated in Service establishments,
who were not required to answer the migration questions, and children born
after the specified dates are included in the figures for the administrative
areas in which they were enumerated, but are listed under the heading, "Not
stated and not applicable^\
It will be appreciated that this can only be a partial measure of the
number of people who moved during a period. Anyone who moved more than once
during a period would be counted only once and then as from his usual address
on the specified date irrespective of any intermediate moves he may have made.
INDUSTRIES
The economic activity information collected at the census was in respect
of persons aged 15 years and over, and related to the activity during the week
ended 8th October, 1966, i.e., the week preceding census day.
An employed person was allocated to the particular branch of industry in
which he or she worked, irrespective of his or her personal occupation. The
total persons, therefore, listed under a particular industrial heading may
include a variety of people such as managers, clerks, drivers, fitters, joiners,
canteen assistants, etc. The results are tabulated on the basis of the revised
"Standard Industrial Classification" (H.M.S.O. 1958), which was also used in
the classification of industries at the 1961 Census, thus giving complete
comparison for 1961 and 1966.
In the industries table the population aged 15 years and over is divided
according to economic position as follows:
Occupied or economically active population
In employment/Working population: These are occupied persons working
full-time or part-time during the week preceding census day,
including those temporarily away from work because of holidays,
sickness, strikes, etc.
Out of employment: Economically active persons out of employment
during the whole of the week before the census, or ceasing to be
employed during the week but expecting to work again. Also included
were those who were sick or prevented by disability from seeking
work for the whole of the week preceding the census.
Economically inactive population
Retired: Formerly occupied persons who ceased to be employed before
8th October, 1966, and who were not expected to work again: women
retired due to marriage are excluded.
Students: Persons above the compulsory education age of 15 years,
who were full-time students in educational establishments.
Other persons economically inactive: All other persons without paid
occupation, including housewives.
WORKPLACE AND TRANSPORT TO WORK
In the tabulation of statistics by place of work and area of usual
residence the smallest areal unit for which figures are shown is the adminis-
trative or local authority area. At this level the statistics show the total
working population resident in an area, the number of these persons who work
within the area and the numbers of persons who work in the area but reside in
other areas. Thus, the figures show the outward flow of people who reside in
an area and work elsewhere, and an inward flow of people who work in an area
XVII


---

## Page 40

and reside elsewhere. The difference between the statistics gives a workplace
movement balance which represents, in the main, the daily fluctuation of the
population in an area.
It is necessary to remember that workplace statistics only measure the
flow across local authority boundaries. Workplace movement which takes place
entirely within the boundaries of a local authority area is not revealed.
In addition to the question on workplace, employed persons were asked to
state the main means of transport normally uBed for the longest part, by
distance, of the journey to their place of work. The analysis of the information
derived from this question is based on the area of usual residence and eight
means of travel, covering public transport, private transport and that provided
by employers.
Members of the Armed Forces enumerated in Service establishments have been
excluded from all figures for workplace movement and means of transport to work
although they are included in figures for the total economically active
population resident in an area. Persons working in Northern Ireland but resi-
dent elsewhere, and persons resident in Northern Ireland but working elsewhere,
have also been excluded from workplace movement and means of transport figures.
CONVENTION AS TO DOTS (.) AND DASHES (-)
In the tables, a dot {.) indicates that figures are not available or that
the column heading is not relevant to the particular line on which the dot is
shown; a dash (-) indicates that the quantity is zero or, in the case of rates,
less than half of the final digit.
XVIII


---

## Page 41

PART III
STATISTICAL NOTES
The explanatory notes and definitions in Part II apply equally to the
notes which follow and to the tables in Part IV.
AREA
Excluding the larger rivers, lakes and tideways, the area of Northern
Ireland at Census day was 5,243 square miles or about 16 per cent of the area
of Ireland as a whole. Particulars of the area of each county borough, county
and administrative district are given in Table 4.
POPULATION
The total population enumerated in Northern Ireland on the night of

    Sunday, . 9th October, 1966, was 1,484, 775. . Of this number 723,884 were males
and 760,891 were females. The total is 59,733 or 4.2 per cent more than the
population enumerated at the 1961 Census, and represents an annual increase of

    10,934, compared with average yearly increases of 5,390, 6,463 and 2,134 persons
    during the 1951-61, 1937-51 and 1926-37 intercensal periods, respectively.
Table 2 shows that censuses were taken decennially between 1821 and 1911
and thereafter at irregular intervals. Throughout the range, of enumerations the
highest point was reached in 1841 when, the, population was 1,648,945 and the

    lowest point in 1891 when it fell to 1,236,056. During this 50 year period the
population declined by 412,889 persons, a 25 per cent reduction representing,

    on average, a fall of over 8,000 persons per year. From 1891 to 1937 each
intercensal period showed slight increases, ranging from 0.1 per cent in 1901

    to 1.8 per cent in 1937. For the period 1937-51, which included the war years
1939-45 when emigration abroad was largely suspended, there was an increase of

    91,176 or 7.1 per cent. For the period 1951-61 the increase amounted to 3.9
per cent. Having regard to the different time intervals, the increase for the
latest intercensal period is relatively greater than that for any previous

    period except for the decade 1821-31 when an increase of 193,553 or 14 per cent
was recorded.
Natural Increase and Migration: The figures for natural increase of the
population, i.e. excess of births over deaths, are obtained from the annual
statistics of births and deaths published in the Registrar General*s Annual
Reports. The natural increase for each intercensal period since birth and death
registration commenced in 1864 is shown in Table I, together with the actual
variation in population for each period and the net loss by migration. Figures
for each county borough and county in respect of the last intercensal period
are given in Table 3.

XIX


---

## Page 42

Natural increase is by far the more important factor in population change

    and, except for the post-famine intercensal periods 1871-81 and 1881-91 during
which emigration was particularly high, has always offset net migration. During
the latest intercensal period the natural increase was almost three times as
great as the net migration.
The increase in the population, on an annual average basis, is the highest
for any intercensal period since 1821-31. It will also be seen from Table I
that this larger addition to the population is directly attributable to a rise

    in the flow of births. In the intercensal period 1961-66 there were 182,489
live births, which is considerably higher (allowing for the different time
intervals) than the number for any intercensal period since that for the period
1871-81.
The annual average number of deaths during the period 1961-66 was only
slightly greater than that for the intercensal period 1951-61. Thus, with
deaths generally stable and births increased the natural increase has risen

    from a yearly average of 14,575 for the period 1951-61 to 17,835 for the latest
intercensal period, the highest ever recorded since the introduction of birth
and death registration.
The loss by migration may be deduced by subtraction of the intercensal
increase from the natural increase. In the five years 1961-66 the net loss by
migration amounted to 37,701, which offsets to some degree the gain resulting
from the natural increase. This net loss by migration represents an annual

    average of 6,901. The comparable annual averages for the periods 1951-61 and
    1937-51 were 9,185 and 4,768, respectively, but, as previously mentioned, the
position in the 1937-51 period was affected by the second world war when
migration abroad was largely suspended.
The migration figures for each county borough and county given in Table 3
represent the net result of movements during the 1961-66 period not only as
between a particular area and countries outside Northern Ireland but also as
between the county borough or county and similar administrative divisions of
Northern Ireland. The extent and direction of either type of movement are not
revealed. The general pattern, however, follows that of the two previous
intercensal periods, showing net movement into Counties Antrim and Down and
outwards from the other divisions.
Intercensal Estimates: The population at the middle of each year is
estimated for various purposes including the calculation of marriage, birth
and death rates. Briefly, such estimates are compiled by taking the population
figures of the preceding census and adjusting them to have regard to the numbers
of births and deaths subsequently registered and the assessed migration. The
following table shows the mid-year estimates from 1961 to 1966.

Density of population: The density of population in Northern Ireland at

    the 1966 Census was 283 persons per square mile compared with 272 in 1961,
    262 in 1951 and 240 in 1926.

County Boroughs and Counties: Table III compares the populations by County
Borough and County at each census from 1901. It will be seen that from 1961 to
1966 the larger population expansions have occurred in the two counties -
Antrim and Down - which border on Belfast. Further, these two counties are the
only main administrative divisions of the country to keep, all their natural
increase. The population of County Antrim increased by 40,066 or 14.6 per cent,
which represents, when the natural increase of 22,681 is deducted, an inward
net migration amounting to 17,405. The population of County Down increased by
19,692 (7.4 per cent) which, with a natural increase of 15,480 shows that there
was a net gain of 4,212 persons by migration. All other counties, with the
exception of County Fermanagh, shared in the increase of population. Counties
Armagh and Londonderry (excluding Londonderry C.B.) had increases of 6.4 and
6.7 per cent, respectively; while Tyrone with an increase of 1.6 per cent, was
the only county with an increase below the national average. The population of
County Fermanagh fell by 1,645 or 3.2 per cent. The population of this county
has been steadily falling since 1841, and has decreased by over 23 per cent
since the beginning of the present century. Londonderry County Borough increased
its population by 3.6 per cent to 55,694 and is the only main administrative
division of the country to have an increased population at each census since
1901.
The population of Belfast County Borough (398,405), which decreased by
6.3 per cent during the ten year period 1951-61, shows a further reduction of
17,451 or 4.2 per cent over the latest intercensal period. Between 1901 and

    1951 the population of Belfast increased by 94,491 or just over 27 per cent.
The decreases recorded for the two most recent intercensal periods are there-
fore in marked contrast with the previous continuous growth of the city but
do not represent a true picture of Belfast as the centre of an urbanised area
extending beyond the legal boundaries of the City.

## Page 46

Table IV shows the population of Belfast County Borough and its environs;
the latter have been taken as Newtownabbey Urban District and those portions
of the Rural Districts of Castlereagh, Hillsborough and Lisburn which comprise
continuous built-up areas extending from the City boundaries. It will be seen
that the population of this urbanised area amounted to 549,139 persons, an
increase of 20,448 or 3.9 per cent since the 1961 census. The population of

    the environmental areas alone increased from 112,835 in 1961 to 150,734 in 1966,
reflecting the extensive housing development in areas close to Belfast, and in
turn explaining the reduction of over 17,000 in the population of the City.
The extent of the movement from Belfast to suburban areas outside the City can

    be judged from Table 12 which shows that during the 1961-66 intercensal period
27,067 persons moved from Belfast to reside in the adjoining local authorities
areas of Newtownabbey Urban District and the Rural Districts of Castlereagh,
Hillsborough and Lisburn.

appropriate to take account of boundary changes during the intercensal period.

The administrative areas with outstanding numerical and relative increases

    were Lisburn R.D. (+ 15,559: 30.4 per cent), Newtownabbey U. D. (+ 9,936: 26.5

differential population changes in these areas is attributable to movement
from Belfast County Borough to these adjoining areas.
Of the 34 urban areas (excluding the two county boroughs) 29 show
increases in population, the largest increase being recorded in Newtownabbey
XXIV


---

## Page 47

Urban District which now has a population of 47,384. Whitehead Urban District
and Lisburn Municipal Borough had increases of 26.4 and 21.6 per cent,
respectively. A further 12 areas had increases of over 10 per cent, ranging
from 17.4 per cent in Tandragee Urban District to 10.4 per cent in Warrenpoint
Urban District. In 5 urban areas the population decreased between 1961 and
1966; the decrease in each of these areas was numerically small.

    Of the 31 Rural Districts, 17 show increases, one is unchanged and 13
show decreases. The districts showing the greatest increases are Lisburn,

    Castlereagh and Hillsborough with increased proportions of 30.4, 24.1 and 18.5
per cent, respectively.
Individually, Lisburn Rural District with a population of 66,686 has the
highest total for any administrative area outside the Belfast County Borough.
At the other end of the scale, 5 urban districts and one rural district have
each a population below 5,000.
Towns of Undefined Boundary: Table 4 includes figures of population,
dwellings, households etc., for 34 identifiable towns or villages which have
no legally defined boundaries and had populations of 1,000 and over at the
1966 Census. Two of these towns, Antrim and Greenisland, had populations of
over 5,000. Comber and Magherafelt each had populations in excess of 3,000.
Populations of over 2,000 were recorded in each of the towns of Ballynahinch,
Bessbrook, Coalisland, Killyleagh and Randalstown. There were also a further

    156 towns with populations less than 1,000 but containing 50 or more houses.
Urban and Rural Aggregates: Table VI shows the changes in the number of
administrative urban and rural areas at each census since 1901, together with
the variations in population in the two types of areas. The figures relate to
the position at each census date and are therefore unadjusted in respect of
any subsequent alterations in area types or boundaries.

It will be seen from Table VI that from the beginning of the century the
proportions of the population living in urban areas increased at each census
until 1961. The 1966 figures show that the proportion residing in urban areas

    has declined since 1961 from 54.0 per cent to 53.2 per cent with a complemen-
tary increase in rural districts from 46.0 to 46.8 per cent. It is noteworthy
that this is the first occasion since such figures were compiled that the rural
section has gained at the expense of the urban areas. The increase of the
population of rural areas does not indicate any return of population to farming
or agricultural pursuits, but merely a movement of population away from their
workplaces in towns to more open residential areas in the surrounding country.
The decrease in the population of Belfast and the increase in that of the
Belfast environs is an indication of this change. Further^ it should be
remembered that certain rural districts now contain more and larger towns of
undefined boundary, villages and areas which are urban in character.
The urban/rural aggregates analysis in Table VII illustrates in another
way the extent to which the population resides in urbanised areas and the
growth of such areas outside the confines of administrative urban districts.

Table VI shows that the proportions of the population living in adminis-
trative Urban Districts and in administrative Rural Districts were 53 and 47
per cent, respectively. On the basis of the urban/rural divisions shown in
Table VII the share of the population disposed in the wider field of urbanised
areas was 69 per cent or a total of 1,027,122 persons, while the population in
the adjusted rural areas amounted to 457,653 persons or 31 per cent of the
total population. A comparison of the 1961 and 1966 enumerations on this
adjusted urban/rural basis reveals that between 1961 and 1966 the urbanised
population increased by 75,935 (8.0 per cent) and the completely rural
population decreased by 16,202 (3.4 per cent).
PRIVATE DWELLINGS AND HOUSEHOLDS
Total Dwellings: At the Census of 1966 Northern Ireland had a total of

    418,806 private dwellings of which 397,387 were inhabited and 21,419 uninha-
    bited. The total at the 1961 Census was 387,315, comprising 369,488 inhabited
and 17,827 uninhabited dwellings. The intercensal increase amounted to
31,491 or 8.1 per cent. It should be noted that this is a net increase and
represents the number of new houses in excess of those demolished or converted
and used for purposes other than dwellings. Table VIII compares the number of
houses in the main administrative divisions in 1961 and 1966.
Each county borough and county shared in the increase in the number of
dwellings. The most outstanding increases were in Counties Antrim and Down,

    the figures being 13,088 (17.4 per cent) and 8,415 (ll.O per cent), respec-
tively. Together the increases in these counties accounted for over 68 per
cent of the excess for the country as a whole. Increases for Counties Armagh
and Londonderry were also relatively high, being above the national average.
The smallest relative gains in the number of dwellings were recorded in
XXVI


---

## Page 49

Belfast County Borough and County Fermanagh. Both these areas suffered losses
in population, and additionally, there has been extensive demolition of houses,
for slum clearance purposes, in Belfast during the latest intercensal period.

As constituted at the date of the 1966 Census,
Table 4 shows the number of private dwellings {inhabited and uninhabited)
in each administrative area. The total number of private dwellings in each of
the 34 Municipal Boroughs and Urban Districts increased during the period

    1961-66. Of the 31 Rural Districts, 19 had increases and 12 had decreases. The
decreases in the 12 Rural Districts were numerically small in each case and
they occurred in districts which also showed decreases in population. The areas
with the largest increases were Lisburn Rural District (+ 4,334; 33.1 per cent),
Newtpwnabbey Urban District (+ 3,127; 29.8 per cent), Castlereagh Rural District

    (+ 3,038; 30.0 per cent) and Hillsborough Rural District {+ 1,905; 25.7 per
cent). These four areas border on Belfast County Borough, and the figures
illustrate the extent of the development which has taken place in the environs
of Belfast during the latest intercensal period. Together, the increases in
these areas accounted for over 39 per cent of the total increase in the number
of dwellings for Northern Ireland. Increases in the total number of dwellings
were also relatively high in Lisburn and Lurgan Municipal Boroughs, Antrim
Rural District and Bangor Municipal Borough, which had excesses amounting to

    27.1, 19.1, 18.5 and 16.6 per cent, respectively, as compared with the national
average of 8.1 per cent.
Uninhabited Dwellings: Uninhabited dwellings numbered 21,419 or 5.1 per
cent of the total dwellings compared with 17,827 uninhabited dwellings and
4.6 per cent in 1961. Of the main administrative divisions, the 1966 propor-
tions ranged from 2.8 per cent in Londonderry County Borough to 6,6 per cent
in County Down. In general, the proportion of dwellings uninhabited in rural
districts is greater than that in urban areas. The marked difference in the
proportion for Belfast County Borough (4.2 per cent) and that for Londonderry
County Borough is probably due to a number of houses in Belfast which were
habitable but vacated prior to demolition in connection with slum clearance
or development schemes or conversion for commercial or other purposes.
It should he noted that dwellings classed as uninhabited included an
unspecified number of houses from which the usual occupants were merely
XXVII


---

## Page 50

temporarily absent on Census night. Other uninhabited dwellings were presumably
vacant for diverse reasons: these would include houses vacated because they
were scheduled for demolition, off-season holiday accommodation, houses vacant
between changes of occupancy and newly completed houses awaiting occupancy.
Inhabited Dwellings: Table 5 shows for Northern Ireland, each county
borough, county and administrative area an analysis of inhabited private
dwellings by the number of rooms and persons therein. Table IX summarises the
figures for Northern Ireland with comparisons for previous censuses.

There were 397,387 inhabited private dwellings recorded in Northern Ireland

    in 1966, an increase of 27,899 or 7.6 per cent above the 1961 figure. The
increase in the number of inhabited dwellings of 7.6 per cent compares with an
increase of 4.6 per cent in the population in private dwellings, indicating a
reduction in the average number of persons per dwelling. It will be seen from
Table IX that the average number of persons per dwelling and per room has fallen

    steadily at each census since 1926 and now stands at a figure of 3.63 and 0.77
persons, respectively.
The figures in Table IX reveal the striking reduction which has been
effected since 1951 in the number of dwellings shared by two or more households.

    Between 1951 and 1961 the number of such dwellings fell from 6,307 to 2,094,
and in 1966 there was a further reduction to 999 which amounts to only 0.3 per
cent of the total inhabited dwellings. In 1966 only 27 dwellings with less than
four rooms were occupied by two or more households, as compared with 900 in

    1926, 388 in 1951 and 88 in 1961. As the size of the dwellings, in terms of
rooms, increases, so does the sharing of dwellings. Thus, for 4 roomed dwellings
the number shared was 139 (O.1 per cent); for 5 roomed dwellings 132 (O. 1 per
cent); for 6 roomed dwellings 191 (0.4 per cent); for 7-8 roomed dwellings
253 (0.9 per cent); and for dwellings larger than this, the number shared was
257 (3.4 per cent).
The percentages of inhabited dwellings by room categories, together with
average population densities per dwelling and per room, are shown in Table X
for each of the main administrative divisions of the country and for the urban
and rural area aggregates.

Of the 397,387 inhabited dwellings recorded at the Census, 1,324 or 0.3
per cent consisted of one room, 13,111 or 3.3 per cent had two rooms, 48,183

    or 12.1 per cent had three rooms, 127,706 or 32.2 per cent had four rooms,
    125,356 or 31.6 per cent had five rooms and the remaining 81,707 or 20.6 per
cent had six or more rooms. If it can be taken that dwellings of 1-3 rooms are
small in size, that those containing 4-5 rooms are medium sized and those with
6 or more rooms come into the large sized category, it can then be said that
15.8 per cent of Northern Ireland's dwellings were small, 63.7 per cent were
medium sized and 20.6 per cent were large sized. It can be seen from Table IX
that dwellings in the small category have decreased at each census since 1926.
The medium sized dwellings have, over the same period, increased both .
numerically and proportionately. Dwellings which fall into the large category
have increased in number at each census since 1926 but, proportionately, have
progressively decreased. Accordingly the average number of rooms per dwelling

    has risen from 4.47 in 1926 to 4.70 in 1966 with the consequent improvement in
density standards illustrated in Table IX.
The percentages of inhabited dwellings by differing sizes vary consider-
ably from one main administrative division to another, as may be seen from
Table X. Of the 1-3 room (small) class of dwelling, the more rural counties of

    Fermanagh, Londonderry and Tyrone have the greatest proportions (24.5, 20.4
and 23.6 per cent, respectively) of dwellings falling into this class. These

    counties also had the highest proportions (27.8, 24.1 and 24.1 per cent,
respectively) of the larger (6 and more rooms) dwellings. Londonderry County
Borough had only 9 per cent of its dwellings in the small dwelling category
compared with 14 per cent in Belfast County Borough. In contrast to the
position obtaining in the three counties named above, with high proportions of
both small and large dwellings, Londonderry County Borough had a much higher
proportion (20.8 per cent) of large dwellings than had Belfast County Borough
(17.6 per cent). County Antrim, with 13.2 per cent of its dwellings falling
into the small category, had an above average proportion of large dwellings
(22 0 per cent). The reverse was the case in County Armagh in that the propor-
tion of dwellings in the small class (16.5 per cent) was above the national
average and the proportion for large dwellings (17.7 per cent) was below
average. County Down was the only main division with a pattern and figures
similar to those of Northern Ireland.
Londonderry County Borough had the highest proportion (70.2 per cent)
for medium sized (4-5 rooms) dwellings. The proportion for Belfast County
XXIX


---

## Page 52

Borough at 68.4 per cent was also well above the national average. The propor-
tions for counties Antrim, Armagh and Down were more or less in line with the
average for the country as a whole at 64 per cent. County Fermanagh had the
lowest proportion (47.7 per cent) of medium sized dwellings and the proportions
for counties Londonderry (55.5 per cent) and Tyrone (52.3 per cent) were also
considerably below the national average.
In terms of persons per dwelling and per room, each main administrative
division recorded improved population densities, but those in Belfast County
Borough and in its adjoining Counties of Antrim and Down remained appreciably
below those in Londonderry County Borough and in Counties Londonderry and
Tyrone. In particular the figures for the two County Boroughs continue to
provide a marked contrast; the densities in Belfast averaged 3.35 persons per
dwelling and 0.73 per room, whereas in Londonderry the averages reached 4.53
and 0. 95, respectively.
Private Households: Table 6 gives details of the number of private
households by household size, rooms occupied and density of room occupation
for each county borough and county and a summary for each administrative urban
and rural district.
In 1966, Northern Ireland had 399,149 private households compared with

    372,793 in 1961. The intercensal increase amounted to 26,356 or 7. 1 per cent,
and it may be contrasted with increases of 4.6 per cent in the private house-
hold population and an increase of 7.6 per cent in the number of inhabited
private dwellings. The notable feature is that the increase in the number of
households far outstripped the growth rate of the population. This is probably
due to changing demographic factors and to improved housing provision. As a
result the average size of household has fallen from 3.70 persons in 1961 to
3.61 persons in 1966 - a reduction of 2.4 per cent.
Turning to the various sizes of households, one-person households increased
by 22.6 per cent and two-person households by 11.5 per cent; together, the
increase in the one- and two-person households accounted for almost three-
quarters of the total increase in the number of households. Households in the
size categories of three to seven persons each increased in number, the
increases ranging from 4.0 per cent for four-person households to 1.8 per cent
for seven-person households. Eight-person households decreased by 2.2 per cent,
and households larger than this increased by 7.0 per cent. Table XI illustrates
the changes in household size in the latest intercensal period.

It can be seen that in 1961 the two-person household group was the
largest and accounted for 22.6 per cent of all households, and that three-
person households came next in numerical order with 19.8 per cent. Four- five-

    and one-person households followed in that order with 17.3, 11.6 and 11.5 per
cent, respectively, and the remaining 17.2 per cent was made up of households
XXX

## Page 53

comprising six or more persons. The same order of preponderance was maintained
in 1966 except that the one--person household group replaced the five-person
category as fourth in numerical importance.
Two-person households accounted for 23.5 per cent of the total households
in 1966. It is significant that the gap between two- and three-person house-

    holds had widened from 2,8 per cent in 1961 to 4.6 per cent in 1966, the
proportion for three-person households falling to 18.9 per cent. The proportion
for four-person households fell to 16.8 per cent, while that for single-person
households increased to 13.1 per cent. Five-person households also fell
proportionately to 11.1 per cent, and the proportion for the remaining cate-
gories of six or more persons fell to 16.6 per cent.
While each of the main administrative divisions of the country shared in
the increase in the number of households, the increases were not uniformly
spread. Table XII shows the variations:

The intercensal increases in Belfast County Borough and County Fermanagh
were very marginal, being in both cases less than one per cent. It should,
however, be remembered that both these areas suffered a loss in population.
The increases in Londonderry County Borough and County Tyrone of under four
per cent were also comparatively small. The largest increases were recorded
in Counties Antrim and Down with 17.1 and 10. 8 per cent, respectively. It is
noteworthy that these counties adjoin Belfast County Borough, and that the
areas within these counties which border on Belfast (Lisburn Rural District
and Newtownabbey Urban District in the case of Antrim, and Hillsborough and
Castlereagh Rural Districts in County Down) had the highest increases of ^
population of all administrative urban and rural districts. Together, the
increases in these four areas amounted to just over 45 per cent of the total
for Northern Ireland. This indicates, to some degree, the extent of the
development which has taken place in the environs of Belfast.
Table XIII shows the comparative distribution of households by size for
county boroughs, counties and the aggregates of the urban and rural areas.
In general, the 1961-66 trends for the main administrative divisions
follow those for Northern Ireland in that the proportions for one- and two-
person households increased in all areas. The proportions for three-person
households decreased in all areas except that for County Fermanagh which
increased marginally by 0.2 per cent. The average size of household fell in
all areas except in Londonderry County Borough where the average number of

    persons per household rose from 4.34 in 1961 to 4.35 in 1966, It is signifi-
cant that in this County Borough the proportions of households in the one to
four-person groups are below the national average and those in the five and
over - person categories are considerably above the average for the country.
