Rooms an d Density of Occupation: In 1966 a total of 399,149 households
occupied 1,868,794 rooms -• an average of 4.68 rooms per household compared with

    averages of 4,60 in 1961 and 4.44 in-1951.
It will be seen from Table 6 that there was a total of 1,867 households
each in occupation of one room in 1966. The majority of these households
(almost 82 per cent) contained either one or two persons, and 7.6 per cent were
households of three persons. The overall proportion of households in occupation
of a single room was 0,5 per cent compared with 0.7 per cent in 1961. Two-roomed
households numbered 14,171 (3.6 per cent). Of these approximately three-quarters
were households of one and two persons, a ten-ch were households of three
persons and the remainder were households of four or more persons. There were

    48,795 (12.2 per cent) households each occupying three rooms; 70 per cent were
households of one to three persons, a further 12 per cent were four-person
households. Almost two-thirds of the households in the country each occupied
four or five rooms. Households of four rooms numbered 127,830 or 32,0 per cent,
while the number of five-roomed households was^ 125,350 or 31.4 per cent. Just
over one-fifth of the households in Northern Ireland each occupied 6 rooms or
more. Small households of one and two persons formed about 39 per cent of the
households occupying four rooms, households of three and four persons accounted
for another 36 per cent and households of five persons amounted to just over
10 per cent. The corresponding figures for households occupying five rooms
were: households of one and two persons, 29 per cent; households with three
and four persons, 40 per cent; and those with five persons, almost 14 per cent.
It is noteworthy that, with the exception of one- and tvio-roomed households,
the two-person household was the most common size of household in occupation
of all sizes of accommodation. In the case of single and two-roomed units,
one-person households were the most common.
The 1966 comparative distribution of households by rooms occupied is
shown in Table XIV for the county boroughs, counties and the aggregates of the
administrative urban and rural districts.
liouseholds occupying one to three rooms were most common in the less
urbanised counties of Fermanagh and Tyrone. In the case of four-room occupa-
tion, only the county boroughs of Belfast and Londonderry had proportions
above the national average - although the proportion for Belfast County
XXXII

Borough was remarkably high at 40.5 per cent; in general, however, the
variations between the main administrative divisions are less marked than in
the small sized households of one to three rooms. Five-roomed accommodation
was proportionately greater in Counties Antrim, Down and Armagh in that order
and lowest in Counties Fermanagh and Tyrone.

It is shown that, during the post-war years, the proportion of households

    occupying one - three rooms has declined from 25.7 per cent in 1951 to 19.7
    per cent in 1961, followed by a further decline to 16.3 per cent in 1966, On
the other hand the proportion for households occupying four - five rooms

    increased progressively from 54,5 per cent in 1951 to 59.9 per cent in 1961
and to 63.4 per cent in 1966. Over these periods the proportion of households
occupying six or more rooms has remained stable at around 20 per cent. It is
apparent from these figures that there has been a consolidation of middle
sized accommodation, which, together with a decrease in the average number of
persons per household, has had the effect of raising space standards.
Table 6 gives the distribution of the private households and population
therein by density classes. It shows that for all households, 10,581 or 2.7
per cent were living at densities greater than two persons per room, compared
with 3.7 per cent in 1961 and 6.0 per cent in 1951, If the next two categories

nolds, 65.4 per cent, lived at a density of under one person per room The
corresponding 1961 proportion was 61.3 per cent.
Table 6 also shows that 98,029 persons or 6.8 per cent of the total
population in private households were living at a density of over two persons
per room. In 1961 such persons numbered 117,752 (8.5 per cent). For the
combined category of one to two persons per room the 1966 figure amounted to

    675,649 persons or 46.9 per cent. The 1961 figures for the one to two person
    category were 666,387 and 48.4 per cent. In 1966, persons living at the
density of less than one person per room numbered 668,031 or 46.3 per cent

    compared with 594,315 or 43.1 per cent in 1961.
The variation in population density rates from one main administrative
area to another which has already been commented upon in relation to private
dwellings (see Table X) is again revealed in Table XIV in relation to house-
holds. In the main administrative areas, the proportions of households
living at a density of over two persons per room ranged from 1.5 per cent
in County Antrim, 1.7 per cent in County Down and 2. 2 per cent in the County
Borough of Belfast to 5.6 and 5.7 per cent, respectively, in the County and
County Borough of Londonderry. The proportion of the population living at a
density of over two persons per room was highest in Londonderry County Borough
(12.9 per cent) and lowest in County Antrim (4.0 per cent).

Table 10 gives the number of persons enumerated in each county borough
and county at the 1966 Census with usual addresses outside Northern Ireland,
together with figures for adjustment of the enumerated population to take
account of usual residence in a county borough or county of Northern Ireland
other than that of enumeration. Of the total enumerated population, 9,823 persons

of the main administrative divisions increased and those of the two remaining

upper limit to 1,362 persons in Belfast County Borough and at the lower limit
to 43 persons in County Londonderry.

MIGRATION
As detailed in a previous paragraph (Natural Increase and Migration), the
population figures ascertained at each census enable the net amount of
migration - the balance between inward and outward migration - during the
intercensal period to be calculated for Northern Ireland as a whole and for
each county and each administrative area. The figures derived in this way are
useful in providing a broad picture of the direction and extent of population
movement. Such statistics, however, do not disclose information as to where
the outward migrants go or from where the inward migrants come, nor do they
reveal movement within a particular area.
In order to obtain more detailed statistics of migration, all persons
were required to state their usual addresses at 24th April, 1965, and at
24th April, 1961. The information derived from these questions is shown in
Tables 11 and 12, which, respectively, cover the periods from the above
mentioned dates to census day. The figures relate to the total enumerated
population and have been tabulated on the basis of usual residence. While
numbers are shown for persons whose usual addresses are outside Northern
Ireland, by country of residence, figures are not given for emigration from
Northern Ireland to any other country.

    During the period, 24th April, 1961, and 9th October, 1966 (the 1961-66
period), the number of migrants, excluding the "Not stated and not applicable"
category, within Northern Ireland was 309,714, representing 21 per cent of the
1966 Census resident population. The number of these migrants who moved within
administrative areas was 181,997 or 59 per cent of the total migrants while
those who moved between administrative areas amounted to 127,717 or 41 per cent.

    In the 1965-66 period (24th April, 1965, and 9th October, 1966), the total
migrants numbered 134,772 or 9.1 per cent of the Census resident population.
Of this total 76,960 or 57 per cent moved within administrative areas and
57,812 or 43 per cent moved between areas.
Table XVII gives the numbers of immigrants and emigrants for each of the

    county boroughs and counties for the periods 1961-66 and 1965-66.

It will be seen that, on balance, the movement of the population has been
similar during both periods, with net movements into Counties Antrim, Armagh,
Down and Londonderry, and net movements out of Counties Fermanagh and Tyrone
and the County Boroughs of Belfast and Londonderry. Belfast County Borough
which suffered a substantial loss in population during the 1961-66 intercensal
period provided the largest number of emigrants in both periods with the
migration balances showing losses of 20,719 and 9,268 persons in the periods

    1961-66 and 1965-66, respectively. The excess of emigrants over immigrants was
also relatively high in Londonderry County Borough, amounting to 4.0 per cent
of the census resident population for the period 1961-66 and 2. 6 per cent for
the period 1965-66.

For the 1961-66 period the largest.movements were from Belfast County
Borough to Counties Antrim and Down, 18,977 and 15,429 persons, respectively.
The movements in the opposite direction amounted to 6,387 persons from County
Antrim and 5,353 persons from County Down. The smallest migratory movements
were of 30 and 36 persons from Counties Armagh and Fermanagh, respectively, to
Londonderry County Borough.
Of the 36,399 persons who moved from Belfast County Borough during the
1961-66 period to the other main administrative divisions in Northern Ireland
34,406 or 95 per cent moved to the adjoining Counties of Antrim and Down.
Within these counties, 27,067 persons or 74 per cent of the total movement
from Belfast moved to the four administrative areas directly bordering on
Belfast. The extent of the movement to.each of these four areas was: County
Antrim - Newtownabbey Urban District 6,379 persons; Lisburn Rural District
9,303; County Down - Castlereagh Rural District 7,888; Hillsborough Rural
District 3,497.
An interesting feature of Table XVIII is the comparatively high number of
migrants to the eastern counties of Antrim and Down and Belfast County Borough
from the western counties, of Fermanagh, Londonderry and Tyrone and Londonderry

    County Borough. Of the 17,862 migrants from these western counties 9,880 or 55
per cent moved to the above named eastern divisions, the figures for each of
the western counties being County Fermanagh 1,669 person^ or 64 per cent of
the total migrants from the county. County Londonderry 3,523 (66 per cent).

    County Tyrone 3,442 (58 per cent) and Londonderry County Borough 1,246 (31 per
cent).
Migration between the main administrative divisions during the 1965-66
period was generally in the same direction as that for the 1961-66 period. The
largest movements were from Belfast County Borough (15,504 persons or 94 per
cent of the total migrants from Belfast) to Counties Antrim (7,854) and Down
(7,650). Movements from the western to the eastern counties were again
comparatively high. The total of such migrants was 5,044 persons or 56 per cent
of the total migrants from the western counties. The figures for each of these
divisions were, County Fermanagh, 851.persons (68 per cent); County Londonderry,

    1,703 (64 per cent); County Tyrone, 1,794 (65 per cent); Londonderry County
Borough, 696 (30 per cent).
The movements of population between each of the local administrative
areas can be determined from Tables 11 and 12. The migration balance - the
difference between inward and outward migration - shows that 24 of the 34
municipal boroughs and urban districts had a net inward balance in the 1961-66

    period. For the 1965-66 period 19 such areas had an inward balance. Of the 31
    rural districts, 11 had an inward balance for the 1961-66 period and 12 for the
1965-66 period.
The number of immigrants to Northern Ireland from elsewhere in the British

SEX, AGE AND MARITAL CONDITION
Table 14 shows for Northern Ireland, each county borough and county the
population by sex, individual years of age and marital condition. Table 15
shows similar information for each municipal borough and each urban and rural
district except that the age analysis is by quinquennial groups.
Sex Ratio: There were 37,007 more females than males enumerated at the
1966 Census. Although the female preponderance is slightly greater than the
corresponding female excess of. 36,594 enumerated, in 1961, it represents a
smaller female/male ratio of 1,051 females per 1,000 males than the corres-
ponding ratio of 1,053 females per 1,000 males recorded at each of the last
two censuses.
The proportion of females to males has decreased, more or less steadily,

    from the peak of 1,102 females per 1,000 males in 1881. This has been due to
the preponderance of male births enhanced by a reduced infant mortality,
particularly in respect of males. About the.beginning of the present century

    there were 50-60 more boys born for every 1,000 girls, but most of this excess
was eliminated in the first year of life. At present there are 80-90 more boys born
for every 1,000 girls, and only about one-twelfth is eliminated by differential
infantile mortality. While it is known that more men than women emigrate and
that the death rates for men, particularly in the upper age groups, are higher
than those for women, the ratio of females to males is nevertheless likely to
continue to decline.

The above statement shows separate ratios for certain age groups,
illustrating the trends already mentioned. The age group 0-4 years reflects
the initial preponderance of males over females, due to the persistent excess
of male over female births, which has been increasing steadily through the
years. The excess of males diminishes until in the age group 15-24 females
exceed males for the first time. The next two age groups show more or less
XXXIX


---

## Page 62

progressive increases in the proportion of females, until at age group 65 years
and over females now outnumber males by almost three to two.
The sex distribution differed widely in different parts of the country.
Of the main administrative divisions of the country, Belfast and Londonderry
County Boroughs had. the highest proportions of females, 1,123 and 1,094,
respectively, per 1,000 males. At the other end of the scale. County Fermanagh
had only 941 females per 1,000 males; females were also in the minority in the
counties of Londonderry and Tyrone. High female/male ratios are found in those
areas attractive to the retired and elderly, among whom women, with their
greater longevity^ are much more numerous, and in those areas where the employ--
ment of women is a predominant factor. Areas with low ratios are mainly
agricultural which would indicate that more women than men tend to leave these
areas for employment in the larger towns.
Ages: At previous censuses the age question required the age to be stated
in years and months. On this occasion the date of birth was asked so as to
secure a greater concentration on the enquiry and so avoid loose or approximate
statements. While no doubt this object has been achieved to a large extent, it
was found that a comparatively small number of people could state only the
year of birth. These incomplete statements of dates of birth must have resulted
in some cases of approximations being given. When the numbers enumerated at
each age at the previous census are adjusted for expected variations from
mortality and migration for the intercensal period and are then compared with
the numbers enumerated at this census at.the relative age points a certain

    amount of overloading at ages 30, 38, 50, 58 and 60 years is revealed. For
most purposes the effects of any resultant distortion would be minimal.

XL

## Page 63

It will be seen from Table XIX that the population increase of 59,733 in
the intercensal period v;as not evenly distributed over the various age groups.

    Decreases were recorded in the quinquennial age groups 10-14, 30-34, 35-39 and
    45-49. The decrease in the 10-14 group was due to the fact that there were some
    2,500 fewer births during the years 1952-56 as compared with the years 1947-51
with the result that, in the natural order, there were fewer persons to attain

    the ages of 10-14 at the 1966 Census than in 1961. Of the other age groups
mentioned, while there were fewer births during the relative years compared
with the birth-years for the corresponding 1961 Census age groups, there is no
doubt that emigration during the intercensal period was also a factor in the
reduction of the population in these groups.
The largest increases were of 14,007 and 13,901 persons in the 0-4 and
5-9 age groups, respectively. These increases reflect the increased flow of
births recorded during the past ten years. The increase in the 0-9 age range
represents 46.7 per cent of the net increase in the population at all ages.

    There was also a substantial increase of 12,672 in the 20-24 age group. Although
there was a decrease in the 10-14 age group, the number of young people aged
under 20 years rose by 32,242 or 6.1 per cent.

The total number of women aged 16-44, approximating to the child-bearing
ages above the minimum age of marriage, was 280,133 and is equivalent to 18.9
per cent of the total population. The corresponding figures for 1961 were
273,735 and 19.2 per cent, respectively.

Changes in the age structure of the population are of interest. For

    example, between 1961 and 1966 the proportion of the population aged 10-14
fell from 9.3 to 8.9 per cent; this fall, however, will not be continued
during the period 1967-.71 because the proportion of the population 5-9 years
old had risen from 9.3 to 9.9 per cent.

The proportion of persons in the younger workers' group, 15-44 years,
has been falling steadily since the 1937 Census. During the recent inter-
censal period the proportions for this group fell from 39.4 to 38.8 per cent;

    the fall was steeper for women (39.1 to 38.5) than for men (39.7 to 39.3). In
    the older workers' group (45-64 years), the proportion increased from 18.9
    per cent in 1937 to 21.6 per cent in 1961; during the latest intercensal
period the proportion has fallen to 21.1 per cent. The proportion aged 0-14
has been increasing steadily since 1937 and now stands at 29.6 per cent. The
proportion for the older population (65 years and over) has increased at each

    census since 1926 and rose from 10.1 per cent in 1961 to 10.5 per cent in 1966.
Broadly, these figures show that the population in Northern Ireland contains a
higher proportion of both old and young persons in the dependent age groups,
and a lower proportion of persons in the working or supporting age groups.
The age structure of the population varies throughout the country. The
following statement shows the percentage distribution of the 1966 population
by broad age groups and county boroughs and counties:

It will be seen that in the young persons' group, 0*14 years, the propor-
tions varied from 34. 6 per cent in Londonderry County Borough to 26.7 per cent
in Belfast County Borough. Further, over 30 per cent of the population of
Counties Antrim, Armagh, Londonderry and Tyrone fell into this group.
Turning to the working range age groups. Counties Antrim and Down are the
only main administrative divisions with percentages of persons in the 15-44
age group above the national average. The percentages of persons in the age
group 45-64 are relatively low in Londonderry County and County Borough and
County Antrim compared with the average for Northern Ireland - it is notable
that in these divisions the persons in the 15-44 age group outnumber the
persons in the older group by two to one.
In the 65 years and over group, the proportions range from 8.4 per cent
in Londonderry County Borough to 12.4 per cent in County Fermanagh. Belfast
County Borough and Counties Down and Fermanagh each had relatively high
proportions of persons aged 65 years and over, compared with the national
figure of 10.5 per cent.
Marital Condition: During the 1961-66 intercensal period the number of
persons aged 16.years (the statutory minimum age of marriage) and over

    increased by 52,191 of whom 15,006 were males and 17,185 females. The number
of single persons in this age range decreased by 10,452 (3,954 males and

    6,498 females), and the number married increased by 40,153 (19,691 males and
20,462 females). The niomber of widowers decreased by 807 and widows increased

    by 3,095. Divorced persons who had not remarried increased by 202 (males 76
and females 126). The following statement shows the percentage distribution
of population aged 16 years and over by marital condition at each census from

INDUSTRIES
Table 16 gives for each county borough and county figures of the popula-

small cost on application.
Occupied population: A total of 1,045,863 persons aged 15 years and over
were enumerated in Northern Ireland at the 1966 Census; of these, 611,456
(416,077 males and 195,379 females) were classed as occupied, i.e. those who
were in employment and those who were out of work but seeking employment. The
numbers occupied represent 83.5 per cent of the male population aged 15 years
and over and 35. 7 per cent of all females aged 15 and over. They include
50,281 persons recorded as out of work but seeking work (39,121 males and
11,160 females).

The total male population aged 15 and over rose by 3.2 per cent between

    1961 and 1966, while the proportion occupied decreased from 86.0 per cent in
    1961 to 83.5 per cent in 1966. The female population aged 15 and over increased
during the intercensal period by 3.3 per cent and the proportion occupied rose

    from 35.3 per cent in 1961 to 35.7 per cent in 1966.
The proportions of the occupied males and females under age 20 were lower

    in 1966 than in 1961 (males 69.1 per cent as compared with 76.5 per cent -
    females 64.4 and 69.7 per cent). The proportions in the age group 20-24 were
also slightly lower. To a large extent these differences reflect the extension
of higher education. The changes in the proportions of the male and female
populations within the ages 25-64 were small and in the main reflect changes
in the age structure of the population. Greater variations were, however,
recorded in the case of men and women aged 65 and over. It will be seen that

    the population of men aged 65 and over increased by some 3,000 between 1961
and 1966, while the number occupied fell by over 3,000. In the case of women
the total aged 65 and over increased by just under 9,000, and the total
occupied fell by almost 200. These figures indicate that higher proportions
of men and women are now retiring at or soon after age 65 than in 1961,

Industries: The total population working in all industries increased by

    2.8 per cent, from 545,702 in 1961 tu 561,175, in 1966. The working male
population increased by 0.9 per cent from 373,733 to 376,956; the working
female population rose by 7.1 per cent from 171,969 to 184,219. These figures
exclude the unemployed.

Of the industry orders listed above whose numbers increased during the
1961-66 period, the individual industrial groups within these Orders which
showed the most noticeable increases were: Order IV. - Mineral oil refining

    (M.L.H. 262), persons employed in this group increased from 2 in 1961 to 233
    in 1966, Chemicals and dyes (M.L.H. 271) from 1,770 to 2,661 persons;
Order VI. - Agricultural machinery - except tractors (M.L.H. 331) from 7 to

    157 persons, Metalworking machine tools (M.L.H. 332) from 1 to 106 persons.
Engineers* small tools and gauges (M.L.H. 333) from 4 to 220 persons. Textile

    machinery and accessories (M.L.H. 335) from 5,287 to 6,468 persons. Mechanical
    handling equipment (M.L^H. 337) from 92 to 773 persons. Telegraph and telephone
apparatus (M.L.H. 363) from 8 to 3,926 persons, Radio and other electronic

    apparatus (M.L.H. 364) from 1,868 to 5,195 persons; Order IX. - Cutlery
(M.L.H. 392) from 6. to 330 persons. Metal industries not elsewhere specified

    (M.L.H. 399) from 1,178 to 2,907 persons; Order XVI. - Rubber (M.L.H. 49l)
    from 380 to 2,222 persons. Plastics moulding and fabricating (M.L.H. 496) from
    141 to 542 persons; Order XXII. - Educational services (M.L.H. 872) from
    16,968 to 22,630 persons. Medical and dental services (M.L.H. 874) from
20,075 to 26,030 persons.
Of the above listed industry orders whose numbers decreased, the indivi-
dual industrial groups most affected within these Orders were: Order I. -
Agriculture and horticulture (M.L.H. 001), the number of persons employed in

    this group decreased from 68,987 in 1961 to 53,545 in 1966; Order VII. -
    Shipbuilding and marine engineering (M.L.H. 370) from 22,323 to 12,804 persons;
Order X. - Spinning and doubling of cotton, flax and man-made fibres (M.L.H.

    412) from 13,857 to 10,107 persons. Made-up textiles (M.L.H. 422) from
    12,368 to 6,226 persons, Textile finishing (M.L.H. 423) from 4,690 to 3,047
persons; Order XIII. - Abrasives and building materials, etc., not elsewhere

    specified (M.L.H. 469) from 2,995 to 2,259 persons; Order XIX. - Railways
    (M.L.H, 701) from 2,748 to 996 persons. Road passenger transport (M.L.H. 702)
    from 8,626 to 7,504 persons, Port and inland water transport (M.L.H. 705)
from 4,174 to 3,179 persons. Additionally, despite the fall in the number of
persons employed in the textile industry (Order X.) the number of persons
engaged in the production of man-made fibres (M.L.H. 41l) increased from

    1,769 in 1961 to 5,131 in 1966.
In 1966 the manufacturing industries (Orders III - XVI) accounted for
28.4 per cent of all working males and 38.8 per cent of working females, while

    in 1961 the comparable percentages were 28.1 for males and 42.2 for females.
The industry orders which contained the highest numbers of working males
were agriculture, forestry and fishing (53,119), construction (51,922) and
distributive trades (47,545); collectively, these orders accounted for 40 per
cent of all males. Over 75 per cent of all females were accounted for by
professional and scientific services (35,748), distributive trades (31,112),
miscellaneous services (25,900), textiles (25,613) and clothing and footwear
(20,337).

There is a marked amount of variation among the age distributions of the
individual industry orders from that for all industries shown above. One
noticeable feature is the concentration of 45 and 40 per cent of all males
aged under 25 years employed, respectively, in industry Order IX (Metal goods
not elsewhere specified) and Order XII (Clothing and footwear). Other industry
orders which had higher than average percentages at younger ages and corres-
ponding lower than average percentages at the higher ages include Order XVI
(Other manufacturing industries) and Order IV (Chemicals and allied industries)
in which 82 and 76 per cent, respectively, of all males were under the age of
45 years. In contrast, industry orders with higher than average percentages
at the older ages include Shipbuilding and marine engineering (Order VII) with
53 per cent of all males over 45 years of age and Agricultxire, forestry,
fishing (Order I) with 51 per cent of all males aged over 45 years.
Females in all industries taken together have a noticeably younger age
distribution than males, nearly two-fifths being under 25 years of age.

    Of the 24 industry orders, 16 have more than 39 per cent of all their
females under the age of 25. In five orders, the age distribution is less
concentrated at the younger ages. Agriculture, forestry, fishing (Order I) had
62 per cent of its females over 45 years of age. Miscellaneous services
(Order XXIII) had 42 per cent. Shipbuilding and marine engineering (Order VII),
Textiles (Order X) and Professional and scientific services (Order XXIl) each
had over 30 per cent of their females aged over 45 years.
The number of married women engaged in industry increased by 17,672 or 35
per cent during the 1961-66 intercensal period. The total married women in

    industry in 1966 was 68,402 (22 per cent of all married women) as compared with
    50,730 (18 per cent) in 1961.

It will be seen that the increase in the number of married women in
employment was spread over all age groups, the largest numerical increases

    being 4,863, 4,291 and 3,218 in the age groups 35-44, 45-54 and 25-34
respectively. The 1966 figures show that approximately 3 out of 10 married

    women in the age groups 16-19 and 20-24 were working, 1 in 5 in the age groups
    25-34 and 55-59, 1 in 4 in the age groups 35-44 and 45-54 and 1 in 10 in the
age group 60-64.
WORKPLACE AND TRANSPOBT TO WORK
Workplace: In discussing population movements in an earlier part of this
report it was pointed out that there was a movement of population away from
workplaces in towns to more residential areas in the surrounding country. There
is no doubt that this movement has been gathering momentum in recent years and
it is of interest to those authorities concerned with the planning and develop-
ment of housing and transport needs and other social services to know the
extent and direction of displacement between residence and workplace.
Table 19 analyses this movement by showing the numbers of the working
population living in one administrative area and working in another, and vice
versa, for all combinations of administrative areas. The figures exclude
persons living in Northern Ireland but working elsewhere, and persons working
in Northern Ireland but living elsewhere.
Of the 555,599 persons included in this analysis 366,635 work within the
areas of residence and 188,964 or 34 per cent work outside the areas of
residence.
Table XXIV shows for each administrative area the resident population at
the Census, an analysis of the working population according to displacement of
area of workplace from the area of residence and the estimated day population.
The estimated day population is obtained by increasing or decreasing the
resident population according to the balance of the inward or outward work-
place movements across the administrative area boundary. The figures exclude
those who failed to state their place of work, those who had no fixed place of
work and those who were not required to answer the place of work question.
Generally speaking there is a daily movement of workers from rural areas
into towns. Of the 36 county and municipal boroughs and urban districts, 24
had greater day populations than night populations. Of the 31 rural districts,
29 had outward balances of workers, the two exceptions being the Rural
Districts of Antrim and Clogher. The largest workplace, movements were recorded
in Belfast County Borough with a gross movement of almost 86,000 workers.
Incoming workers numbered 66,958 and outgoing workers 18,680; on balance, the

    day population exceeded the night population by 48,'278 or 12.3 per cent.
Among the other administrative urban areas ther^ were 3 with day popula-
tions exceeding the night populations by more than 3,000 (namely, Londonderry
County Borough with an excess of 3,068 or 5,5 per cent of the resident
population, Ballymena Municipal Borough: 3,701 or 23.4 per cent and Newry
Urban District: 3,705 or 30.3 per cent).
Apart from Belfast County Boroxigh, the largest outward movements were
recorded in Lisburn Rural District (17,609 persons or 26.7 per cent of the
resident population), Castlereagh Rural District (14,571 persons or 31.7 per
cent) and Newtownabbey Urban District (13,033 or 27.6 per cent).. Of the

    combined total (45,213) of outgoing workers from these areas, 37,60^2 or 83
per cent work in the adjoining County Borough.of Belfast, an indication of
the extent these districts serve as dormitory areas for Belfast.
Means of Transport to Work: The statistical data on the displacement
between residence and workplace can be complemented by information on the
means of transport used by workers for their journeys to work.
Table 20 shows for the working population, covered by Table 19, the
number of such persons residing in each administrative area and the means of
transport normally used for the major part of the journey to their place of

## Page 72

work. The figures for Northern Ireland show that 29.3 per cent of the working
population travelled to work by private car, 27.0 per cent used public trans-
port, 20.0 per cent normally walked to work, 5.9 per cent travelled to work by
motor or pedal cycle and 3. 7 per cent were transported by private bus or goods
vehicle. The remaining 14.1 per cent consisted of persons who worked at home,
members of H.M. Forces enumerated in barracks or aboard ships and persons who
failed to answer the transport to work question.
Table XXV shows for each county borough and county the numbers and percen-
tages of workers using the different means of transport to work.

Of the main administrative divisions, Belfast County Borough had the
highest percentage (48.4) of workers using public transport to travel to work.
Londonderry County Borough, with 31.1 per cent of its workers travelling by
public transport, was the only other division with a proportion above the
national average. The percentages of workers using public transport in Counties

    Fermanagh, Tyrone and Armagh were noticeably low at 3.5, 8.9 and 11.1, respec-
tively. Counties Fermanagh and Tyrone are predominantly rural areas with a
large proportion of workers engaged in agriculture, living and working on
farms. County Down had the highest percentage (37.6) of workers using private
cars; the percentages were also high for Counties Antrim (36.4), Londonderry
(30.7) and Armagh (30.3). The percentages of workers travelling by private cars
in the County Boroughs of Belfast and Londonderry were low compared with the
national average. Of the workers who walk to work, Londonderry County Borough
had the highest percentage at 38.4, almost twice the national average.

## Page 252

CENSUS, NORTHERN IRELAND, 1966
HOUSEHOLD RETURN
A
Form issued by the Registrar General pursuant to the Census Act, 1965
NOTICE
L The Head, or person acting as Head, of a private household is required by
law to make a return in this form in respect of a!i persons (members of the
household, including visitors, employees and boarders) who are present at
midnight on the night of Sunday, 9th October, 1966, in the dwelling, and all
persons who arrive at the dwelling and join the household on Monday, lOth
October, 1966, before the collection of the return and who have not been
enumerated elsewhere.
2. If a house or partof a house, flat, apartment, etc., is let or sublet to separate
households then each occupier mu^t make a separate Return applicable to
his part of the accommodation. Boarders are not to be considered as separate
occupiers but as part of the household with which they board,
3. In the case of Hotels, Boarding Houses, Lodging Houses and similar
establishments, the Manager or other person in charge must make a
Return in respect of all persons who spend Census night on the premises or
who arrive there on Monday, lOth October, 1966, and who have not been
enumerated elsewhere.
4. Every person is required by law to give to the person making the Return
such information as may be necessary to enable the Return to be completed.
But should a person object to giving the information in this way he may
obtain from the enumerator or the nearest Police Station a form on which
to make a separate Return, In which case the person responsible for making
the main Return should complete columns A and B only in respect of that
person.
5. This Return must be ready on MONDAY, 10th October. If the answers
appear to the enumerator to be incomplete or inaccurate he must ask any
questions necessary to enable him to correct the Return,
6. The person responsible for making the Return may, if unable to .deliver it
to the enumerator personally, arrange forsome other person to do so on
his behalf. If desired, the Return may be given under cover to such other
person for handing to the enumerator.
7. If any person whose duty it is to make a Return or to give information
refuses to do so, or wilfully gives false information or uses otherwise than
for the purpose of making the Return any information given him for that
purpose, he will be liable on conviction to a fine not exceeding Ten Pounds*.
8. The contents of the Return are strialy confidential.
W* G. NICHOLL,
Registrar General.
TO BE FILLED UP BY '
Census District and Enumeration District Nos.
County or County Borough

Town [and
Town or Village
Street, etc., with

THE EMUMERATOR
/
District Efectoral Divrsion of Ward
N.L Parliamentary Division in County Boroughs
NOTES FOR GUIDANCE IN THE COMPLETION OF THE RETURN
These notes will help you to answer the questions. Please read them and study the
examples before you fill in the answers.
Your co-operation is sought in providing accurate and complete answers to the
questions so that reliable statistics may be prepared. It is the duty of the Enumerator
to examine each Return to see whether It has been properly completed, and it will
assist him In his task if you will exercise care In furnishing replies to the various
questions.
NOTES
L Relationships (Columns A and B)
Where a husband and Ills wife are both included,
write their names on consecutive lines In column A*
A relative visiting the family (e.g., a son on a visit
to his father's house) should be described as a
Visitor. A person residing at a hotel, etc., should
be described as "Resident" or "Boarder"*
2. Date of Birth (Column D)
(G) If the date of birth is not known exactly give
It as accurately as possible.
(b) If the date of birth !s unknown give the ap-*
proximate year of birth.
3. Marital Conditioai (Column B}
(a) If separated and not divorced write "Married".
(fo) If divorced but remarried write "Married".
4* Usual Address (Column F)
(Q) School children, students, etc, who live away
from home during term should give their
home address, not their term-time address.
(b) Members of H.M. Forces should give thet
address of their married quarters or other
home address.
(c) Resident staff should regard the private house,
boarding house or other premises where they
live as their usual address and write "Here".
(d) For persons with no settled address write
"None".
(e) For boarders who have a settled residence with
this household write "Here".
Employment (Columns J» K and t )
S. In lEmployment means that the person had a job
during the week ending Saturday, 8th October,
l%^t even if he was away from work because of
holidays, sickness, strikes, etc. For example, a sick
person is in employment if his job is waiting for him
when he gets well.
Employment Includes any service in the Armed
Forces, and jobs at which a person worked for only
a few hours, e.g«,for a housewife who helped in her
husband's shop OF did some office cleaning.
6.^Sore than one Employment. If the person
changed his employment during the week, give
details of the employment in, the later part of the
week. If the person normally follows more than
one employment during the week, give details of
the main employment only.
7m Unemployed. If a person is unemployed or does
not follow an occupation for payment or profit
write "Out of work", "Retired", "At school",
*'Student", "Home duties", etc., in column J as
applicable.
8. Mace of Work (Column i(i))
(a) For people with no regular place of work
such as sales representatives, transport in-
spectors, certain building workers and others
who do not work daily at or from a fixed
address or depot, write "No fixed place".
(b) For people working daily at or from a fixed
address or depot, such as certain transport
workers and building workers employed on a
site, give the address of the depot, site or
other fixed address.
(c) For seamen give the name of the ship and, if it
is in the United Kingdom, the port in which it
Is lying, otherwise give the name of the home
port.
t
I
9. Employer and employer's business (Column
J(ii))
These details are required only to help In classifying
the industry or service. Describe the business fully
and avoid using initials or abbreviations. Such
terms as "Manufacturer'*, "Merchant", **Agent'*,
"Broker", "Factor", "Dealer", "Engineering"* "Re-,
tailer", "Shopkeeper" are not enough by themselves
and further details should be given about the
articles manufactured or dealt in.
For civil servants, local government officers and
other public officials give the name of the Govern-
ment Department or Local Authority and the
branch in which they are employed.
For people employed solely in private domestic
service there Is no need to give the names of
individual employers during the week; |t is enough
to write "Private" in answer to this question.
10. FuU-.time/Part«time (Column K)
(o) If the employment is normally full-time but
was interrupted during the week (for example
by sickness, injury, holidays, short-time work-
ings, strikes or unfavourable weather) or was
started or stopped part way through the week
write "Full-time".
(6) For persons who regularly work less than the
normal hours in employment write "Part-time".
(c) For part-time workers not at work during the
week ending 8th October, 1966, write "Out of
work" in column J.
i L Transport to Work (Column L)
(o) For a person using more than one method
of transport to work give only the method by
which the longest distance is travelled; for
example, if the normal journey to work is one
mile by bus and five miles by train, write
"Train".
(b) For a person whose main method of transport
to work is by bus write "Public service bus"or
"Private bus" (including employer's lorry or
van) whichever is appropriate.
(c) For a person who travels to wor^ by private car
write "Private car, driver" or "Private car,
passenger" whichever is appropriate.
(d) For a person whose main method of transport
to work is by motor cycle combination write
"Motor cycle combination" and not "Motor
cycle".
(e) For a person who works at home write "None".
(f) For a person with no fixed place of work
give the method of transport most often used
for going to work.
(g) For a person who walks all or most of the way
to work write "On foot".
